        <a href="/wiki/File:Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg/300px-Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg/450px-Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg/600px-Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Members_of_the_Haitian_National_Police_Force_marching_band_stand_at_parade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Members of the Haitian National Police Force marching band stand at parade
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The legal system is based on a modified version of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encylopedia_Britannica_-_Haiti_21-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encylopedia_Britannica_-_Haiti-21">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Haiti has consistently ranked among the most corrupt countries in the world on the <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">Corruption Perceptions Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup> According to a 2006 report by the <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">Corruption Perceptions Index</a>, there is a strong correlation between corruption and poverty in Haiti. The nation ranked first of all countries surveyed for of levels of perceived domestic corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup> It is estimated that President <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Duvalier" title="Jean-Claude Duvalier">"Baby Doc" Duvalier</a>, his wife <a href="/wiki/Mich%C3%A8le_Bennett" title="Michèle Bennett">Michelle</a>, and their agents stole US $504 million from the country's treasury between 1971 and 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup> Similarly, after the Haitian Army folded in 1995, the Haitian National Police (HNP) gained sole power of authority on the Haitian citizens. Many Haitians as well as observers of the Haitian society believe that this monopolized power could have given way to a corrupt police force.<sup id="cite_ref-Haiti:_Police_and_Law_Enforcement_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haiti:_Police_and_Law_Enforcement-245">[243]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Similarly, some media outlets alleged that millions were stolen by former president <a href="/wiki/Jean-Bertrand_Aristide" title="Jean-Bertrand Aristide">Jean-Bertrand Aristide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-American_Spectator_Vol_1994_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American_Spectator_Vol_1994-246">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup> In March 2004, at the time of Aristide's <a href="/wiki/Kidnapping" title="Kidnapping">kidnapping</a>, a <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> article wrote that the Bush administration State Department stated that Aristide had been involved in drug trafficking.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup> The BBC also described <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_scheme" title="Pyramid scheme">pyramid schemes</a>, in which Haitians lost hundreds of millions in 2002, as the "only real economic initiative" of the Aristide years.<sup id="cite_ref-basketcase_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basketcase-251">[249]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Conversely, according to the 2013 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_on_Drugs_and_Crime" title="United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime">United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime</a> (<a href="/wiki/UNODC" class="mw-redirect" title="UNODC">UNODC</a>) report, murder rates in Haiti (10.2 per 100,000) are far <i>below</i> the regional average (26 per 100,000); less than <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r993651011">.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px;white-space:nowrap}</style><span role="math" class="sfrac nowrap tion" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span class="num" style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="slash sr-only">/</span><span class="den" style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">4</span></span> that of Jamaica (39.3 per 100,000) and nearly 
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r993651011"><span role="math" class="sfrac nowrap tion" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span class="num" style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="slash sr-only">/</span><span class="den" style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span> that of the Dominican Republic (22.1 per 100,000), making it among the safer countries in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> In large part, this is due to the country's ability to fulfil a pledge by increasing its national police yearly by 50%, a four-year initiative that was started in 2012. In addition to the yearly recruits, the Haitian National Police (HNP) has been using innovative technologies to crack down on crime. A notable bust in recent years<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> led to the dismantlement of the largest kidnapping ring in the country with the use of an advanced software program developed by a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">West Point</a>-trained Haitian official that proved to be so effective that it has led to its foreign advisers to make inquiries.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Police_Department" title="New York City Police Department">New York City Police Department</a> (NYPD) sent a team of veteran officers to Haiti to assist in the rebuilding of its police force with special training in investigative techniques, strategies to improve the anti-kidnapping personnel and community outreach to build stronger relationships with the public especially among the youth. It has also helped the HNP set up a police unit in the center of <a href="/wiki/Delmas,_Ouest" title="Delmas, Ouest">Delmas</a>, a neighborhood of Port-au-Prince.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USAID_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-258">[256]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2012 and 2013, 150 HNP officers received specialized training funded by the US government, which also contributed to the infrastructure and communications support by upgrading radio capacity and constructing new police stations from the most violent-prone neighborhoods of <a href="/wiki/Cit%C3%A9_Soleil" title="Cité Soleil">Cité Soleil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grande_Ravine" title="Grande Ravine">Grande Ravine</a> in Port-au-Prince to the new northern industrial park at <a href="/wiki/Caracol,_Nord-Est" title="Caracol, Nord-Est">Caracol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USAID_258-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-258">[256]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haitian_penitentiary_system">Haitian penitentiary system</span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Port-au-Prince" title="Port-au-Prince">Port-au-Prince</a> <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">penitentiary</a> is home to half of Haiti's prisoners. The prison has a capacity of 1,200 <a href="/wiki/Detention_(imprisonment)" title="Detention (imprisonment)">detainees</a> but as of November&nbsp;2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Haiti&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the penitentiary was obliged to keep 4,359 detainees, a 454% occupancy level.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-259">[257]</a></sup> This leads to severe consequences for the inmates. </p>
      <p>One cell could hold up to 60 inmates which was originally designed for only 18, therefore creating tight and uncomfortable living conditions. The inmates are forced to create makeshift <a href="/wiki/Hammock" title="Hammock">hammocks</a> from the wall and ceilings. The men are on a 22/ 23 hour lock up in the cells so the risk of diseases is very high.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-259">[257]</a></sup> Unable to receive sufficient funds from the government as Haiti endures severe natural disasters which takes up their attention and resources, such as the 2010 earthquake, has caused deadly cases of <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, combined with the tight living conditions, increases the risk of infectious diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> which has led to 21 deaths in January 2017 alone at the Port-au-Prince penitentiary.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_259-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-259">[257]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Haitian law states that once arrested, one must go before a judge within 48 hours; however, this is very rare. In an interview with <a href="/wiki/Unreported_World" title="Unreported World">Unreported World</a>, the prison governor stated that around 529 detainees were never sentenced, there are 3,830 detainees who are in prolonged detained trial detention. Therefore, 80% are not convicted.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Unless families are able to provide the necessary funds for inmates to appear before a judge there is a very slim chance the inmate would have a trial, on average, within 10 years. <a href="/wiki/Brian_Concannon" title="Brian Concannon">Brian Concannon</a>, the director of the non-profit Institute for Justice and Democracy in Haiti, claims that without a substantial bribe to persuade judges, prosecutors and lawyers to undergo their case, there is no prospect for getting a trial for years.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Families may send food to the penitentiary; however, most inmates depend on the meals served twice a day. However, the majority of the meals consists of ration supplies of rice, oats or cornmeal, which has led to deadly cases of malnutrition-related ailments such as <a href="/wiki/Thiamine_deficiency" title="Thiamine deficiency">beriberi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anaemia</a>. Prisoners too weak are crammed in the penitentiary infirmary.<sup id="cite_ref-prison_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prison-262">[260]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In confined living spaces for 22–23 hours a day, inmates are not provided with latrines and are forced to <a href="/wiki/Defecation" title="Defecation">defecate</a> into plastic bags and leave them outside their cells. These conditions were considered inhumane by the Inter-American Court of Human Rights in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-prison_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prison-262">[260]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Haiti" title="Economy of Haiti">Economy of Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg/350px-Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg" decoding="async" width="350" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg/525px-Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg/700px-Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2668" data-file-height="2085"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Haiti_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A proportional representation of Haiti's exports
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Haiti has a predominantly free market economy, with a <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> of $19.97 billion and per capita GDP of $1,800 (2017 estimates).<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti_20-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti-20">[19]</a></sup> The country uses the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_gourde" title="Haitian gourde">Haitian gourde</a> as its currency. Despite its tourism industry, Haiti is one of the poorest countries in the Americas, with poverty, corruption, political instability, poor infrastructure, lack of health care and lack of education cited as the main causes.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti_20-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti-20">[19]</a></sup> Unemployment is high and many Haitians seek to emigrate. Trade declined dramatically after the <a href="/wiki/2010_Haiti_earthquake" title="2010 Haiti earthquake">2010 earthquake</a> and subsequent <a href="/wiki/2010_Haiti_cholera_outbreak" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 Haiti cholera outbreak">outbreak of cholera</a>, with the country's <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product#Cross-border_comparison" title="Gross domestic product">purchasing power parity GDP</a> falling by 8% (from US$12.15 billion to US$11.18 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_20110303_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_20110303-4">[4]</a></sup> Haiti ranked 145 of 182 countries in the 2010 United Nations <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>, with 57.3% of the population being deprived in at least three of the HDI's poverty measures.<sup id="cite_ref-UNDP_2010_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP_2010-263">[261]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the disputed 2000 election and accusations about President Aristide's rule,<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup> US aid to the Haitian government was cut off between 2001 and 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> After Aristide's departure in 2004, aid was restored and the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian army">Brazilian army</a> led a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Stabilization_Mission_in_Haiti" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Stabilization Mission in Haiti">United Nations Stabilization Mission in Haiti</a> peacekeeping operation. After almost four years of recession, the economy grew by 1.5% in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup> In September 2009, Haiti met the conditions set out by the <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Heavily_Indebted_Poor_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavily Indebted Poor Countries">Heavily Indebted Poor Countries</a> program to qualify for cancellation of its external debt.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[265]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>More than 90 percent of the government's budget comes from an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Petrocaribe" title="Petrocaribe">Petrocaribe</a>, a Venezuela-led oil alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_aid">Foreign aid</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_aid_to_Haiti" title="Foreign aid to Haiti">Foreign aid to Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Haiti received more than US$4 billion in aid from 1990 to 2003, including US$1.5 billion from the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup> The largest donor is the US, followed by Canada and the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[268]</a></sup> In January 2010, following the earthquake, US President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> promised US$1.15 billion in assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> nations pledged more than €400 million (US$616 million).<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> Neighboring <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> has also provided extensive humanitarian aid to Haiti, including the funding and construction of a public university,<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[271]</a></sup> human capital, free healthcare services in the border region, and logistical support after the 2010 earthquake.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the UN Office of the Special Envoy for Haiti, as of March&nbsp;2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Haiti&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, of humanitarian funding committed or disbursed by bilateral and multilateral donors in 2010 and 2011, only 1% has been pledged to the Haitian government.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[273]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The United Nations states that in total US$13.34 billion has been earmarked for post-earthquake reconstruction through 2020, though two years after the 2010 quake, less than half of that amount had actually been released, according to UN documents. As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Haiti&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the US government has allocated US$4 billion, US$3 billion has already been spent, and the rest is dedicated to longer-term projects.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Former US President Bill Clinton's foundation contributed US$250,000 to a <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> initiative for a sister-program of "Ranmase Lajan" or "Picking Up Money" by use of <a href="/wiki/Reverse_vending_machine" title="Reverse vending machine">reverse vending machines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h3> 
      <p>According to the 2015 CIA <a href="/wiki/World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="World Factbook">World Factbook</a>, Haiti's main import partners are: Dominican Republic 35%, US 26.8%, Netherlands Antilles 8.7%, China 7% (est. 2013). Haiti's main export partner is the US 83.5% (est. 2013).<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[276]</a></sup> Haiti had a trade deficit of US$3 billion in 2011, or 41% of GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-ammart_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ammart-279">[277]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_Haiti" title="Electricity sector in Haiti">Electricity sector in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1925, the city of <a href="/wiki/Jacmel" title="Jacmel">Jacmel</a> was the first area in the Caribbean to have electricity and was subsequently dubbed the <i>City of Light</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Today, Haiti relies heavily on an oil alliance with <a href="/wiki/Petrocaribe" title="Petrocaribe">Petrocaribe</a> for much of its energy requirements. In recent years, hydroelectric, solar and wind energy have been explored as possible sustainable energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG/220px-Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG/330px-Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG/440px-Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="2030" data-file-height="1197"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Power_plant_in_Port-au-Prince.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Power plant in Port-au-Prince
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As of 2017, among all the countries in the Americas, Haiti is producing the least amount of energy. Less than a quarter of the country has electric coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> Most regions of Haiti that do have energy are powered by generators. These generators are often expensive and produce a lot of pollution. The areas that do get electricity experience power cuts on a daily basis, and some areas are limited to 12 hours of electricity a day. Electricity is provided by a small number of independent companies: Sogener, E-power, and Haytrac.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup> There is no national electricity grid within the country.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-284">[282]</a></sup> The most common source of energy used is wood, along with charcoal. In Haiti, about 4 million metric tons of wood products are consumed yearly.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup> Like charcoal and wood, petroleum is also an important source of energy for Haiti. Since Haiti cannot produce its own fuel, all fuel is imported. Yearly, around 691,000 tons of oil is imported into the country.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_284-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-284">[282]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 31 October 2018, Evenson Calixte, the General Director of energy regulation (ANARSE) announced the 24 hour electricity project. To meet this objective, 236 Megawatt needs to installed in Port-au-Prince alone, with an additional 75 Megawatt needed in all other regions in the country. Presently only 27,5% of the population has access to electricity; moreover, the national energy agency l'Électricité d'Haïti (Ed'H) is only able to meet 62% of overall electricity demand said Fritz Caillot, the Minister of Public Works, Transportation and Communication (Travaux publics, transport et communication (TPTC)).<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_income">Personal income</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:CapHaitienMarche.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/CapHaitienMarche.jpg/290px-CapHaitienMarche.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/CapHaitienMarche.jpg/435px-CapHaitienMarche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/CapHaitienMarche.jpg/580px-CapHaitienMarche.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1771" data-file-height="1189"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:CapHaitienMarche.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A market in <a href="/wiki/Cap-Ha%C3%AFtien" title="Cap-Haïtien">Cap-Haïtien</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">The World Factbook</a></i> reports a shortage of skilled labor, widespread unemployment and underemployment, saying "more than two-thirds of the labor force do not have formal jobs." It is also often stated that three-quarters of the population lives on US$2 or less per day.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-287">[285]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i><a href="/wiki/The_CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="The CIA World Factbook">The CIA World Factbook</a></i> also states that "<a href="/wiki/Remittances" class="mw-redirect" title="Remittances">remittances</a> are the primary source of foreign exchange, equaling one-fifth (20%) of GDP and representing more than five times the earnings from exports in 2012".<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup> The World Bank estimates that over 80% of college graduates from Haiti were living abroad in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Occasionally, families who are unable to care for children financially may send them to live with a wealthier family as a <i><a href="/wiki/Restavek" title="Restavek">restavek</a></i>, or <a href="/wiki/House_servant" class="mw-redirect" title="House servant">house servant</a>. In return the family are supposed to ensure that the child is educated and provided with food and shelter, however the system is open to abuse and has proved controversial, with some likening it to child slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-kennedy14_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy14-290">[288]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Real_estate">Real estate</span></h3> 
      <p>In rural areas, people often live in wooden huts with corrugated iron roofs. Outhouses are located in back of the huts. In Port-au-Prince, colorful <a href="/wiki/Shantytowns" class="mw-redirect" title="Shantytowns">shantytowns</a> surround the central city and go up the mountainsides.<sup id="cite_ref-c151222_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c151222-292">[290]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The middle and upper classes live in suburbs, or in the central part of the bigger cities in apartments, where there is urban planning. Many of the houses they live in are like miniature fortresses, located behind walls embedded with metal spikes, barbed wire, broken glass, and sometimes all three. The gates to these houses are barred at night, the house is locked; guard dogs patrol the yard. These houses are often self-sufficient as well. The houses have backup generators, because the electrical grid in Haiti is unreliable. Some even have rooftop reservoirs for water, as the water supply is also unreliable.<sup id="cite_ref-c151222_292-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c151222-292">[290]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Haiti" title="Agriculture in Haiti">Agriculture in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Amid_rows_of_cabbage,_Haiti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Amid_rows_of_cabbage%2C_Haiti.jpg/300px-Amid_rows_of_cabbage%2C_Haiti.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Amid_rows_of_cabbage%2C_Haiti.jpg/450px-Amid_rows_of_cabbage%2C_Haiti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Amid_rows_of_cabbage%2C_Haiti.jpg/600px-Amid_rows_of_cabbage%2C_Haiti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1328"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Amid_rows_of_cabbage,_Haiti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Rows of cabbage, Haiti
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Haiti is the world's leading producer of <a href="/wiki/Chrysopogon_zizanioides" title="Chrysopogon zizanioides">vetiver</a>, a root plant used to make luxury perfumes, essential oils and fragrances, providing for half the world's supply.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup> Roughly 40–50% of Haitians work in the agricultural sector.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti_20-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti-20">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-feedh_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feedh-296">[294]</a></sup> Haiti relies upon imports for half its food needs and 80% of its rice.<sup id="cite_ref-feedh_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feedh-296">[294]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Haiti exports crops such as <a href="/wiki/Mango" title="Mango">mangoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cacao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papaya" title="Papaya">papayas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahogany" title="Mahogany">mahogany</a> nuts, <a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Watercress" title="Watercress">watercress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ute.gouv.ht_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ute.gouv.ht-297">[295]</a></sup> Agricultural products comprise 6% of all exports.<sup id="cite_ref-ammart_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ammart-279">[277]</a></sup> In addition, local agricultural products include <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beans" class="mw-redirect" title="Beans">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pistachio" title="Pistachio">pistachios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_pea" title="Pigeon pea">pigeon peas</a>, sugarcane, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ute.gouv.ht_297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ute.gouv.ht-297">[295]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency">Currency</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haitian_gourde" title="Haitian gourde">Haitian gourde</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Haitian_gourde" title="Haitian gourde">Haitian gourde</a> (HTG) is the national currency. The "<a href="/wiki/Haitian_gourde" title="Haitian gourde">Haitian dollar</a>" equates to 5 gourdes (<i>goud</i>), which is a fixed exchange rate that exists in concept <i>only,</i> but are commonly used as informal prices.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The vast majority of the business sector and individuals in Haiti will also accept US dollars, though at the outdoor markets gourdes may be preferred. Locals may refer to the USD as "dollar américain" (<i>dola ameriken</i>) or "dollar US" (pronounced <i>oo-es</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Haiti" title="Tourism in Haiti">Tourism in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Labadee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Labadee.jpg/290px-Labadee.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Labadee.jpg/435px-Labadee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Labadee.jpg/580px-Labadee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2244" data-file-height="1011"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Labadee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Labadee" title="Labadee">Labadee</a>, a cruise ship destination
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The tourism market in Haiti is undeveloped and the government is heavily promoting this sector. Haiti has many of the features that attract tourists to other Caribbean destinations, such as white sand beaches, mountainous scenery and a year-round warm climate, however the country's poor image overseas, at times exaggerated, has hampered the development of this sector.<sup id="cite_ref-Encylopedia_Britannica_-_Haiti_21-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encylopedia_Britannica_-_Haiti-21">[20]</a></sup> In 2014, the country received 1,250,000 tourists (mostly from cruise ships), and the industry generated US$200 million in 2014.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Several hotels were opened in 2014, including an upscale <a href="/wiki/Best_Western#Differentiation" title="Best Western">Best Western Premier</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[299]</a></sup> a five-star Royal Oasis hotel by Occidental Hotel and Resorts in <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9tion-Ville" title="Pétion-Ville">Pétion-Ville</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[302]</a></sup> a four-star <a href="/wiki/Marriott_International" title="Marriott International">Marriott Hotel</a> in the Turgeau area of Port-au-Prince<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup> and other new hotel developments in Port-au-Prince, <a href="/wiki/Les_Cayes" title="Les Cayes">Les Cayes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cap-Ha%C3%AFtien" title="Cap-Haïtien">Cap-Haïtien</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacmel" title="Jacmel">Jacmel</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Carnival" title="Haitian Carnival">Haitian Carnival</a> has been one of the most popular carnivals in the Caribbean. In 2010, the government decided to stage the event in a different city outside Port-au-Prince every year in an attempt to decentralize the country.<sup id="cite_ref-haitilibre.com_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haitilibre.com-306">[304]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[305]</a></sup> The National Carnival&nbsp;– usually held in one of the country's largest cities (i.e., Port-au-Prince, Cap-Haïtien or Les Cayes)&nbsp;– follows the also very popular Jacmel Carnival, which takes place a week earlier in February or March.<sup id="cite_ref-haitilibre.com_306-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haitilibre.com-306">[304]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caracol_Industrial_Park">Caracol Industrial Park</span></h3> 
      <p>On 21 October 2012, Haitian President <a href="/wiki/Michel_Martelly" title="Michel Martelly">Michel Martelly</a>, US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, Bill Clinton, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Stiller" title="Ben Stiller">Ben Stiller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sean_Penn" title="Sean Penn">Sean Penn</a> inaugurated the 600 acres (240&nbsp;ha) Caracol industrial park, the largest in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-308">[306]</a></sup> Costing US$300 million, the project, which includes a 10-megawatt <a href="/wiki/Power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Power plant">power plant</a>, a water-treatment plant and worker housing, is intended to transform the northern part of the country by creating 65,000 jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_308-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-308">[306]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The park is part of a "master plan" for Haiti's North and North-East departments, including the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Cap-Ha%C3%AFtien_International_Airport" title="Cap-Haïtien International Airport">Cap-Haïtien International Airport</a> to accommodate large international flights, the construction of an international seaport in <a href="/wiki/Fort-Libert%C3%A9" title="Fort-Liberté">Fort-Liberté</a> and the opening of the $50 million Roi Henri Christophe Campus of a new university in Limonade (near Cap-Haïtien) on 12 January 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[307]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korean</a> clothing manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Sae-A_Trading_Co._Ltd" class="mw-redirect" title="Sae-A Trading Co. Ltd">Sae-A Trading Co. Ltd</a>, one of the park's main tenants, has created 5,000 permanent jobs out of the 20,000 projected and has built 8,600 houses in the surrounding area for its workers. The industrial park ultimately has the potential to create as many as 65,000 jobs once fully developed.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[308]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[309]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Haiti" title="Transport in Haiti">Transport in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg/290px-Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg/435px-Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg/580px-Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg 2x" data-file-width="779" data-file-height="580"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Haiti_rail_map_1925.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Rail map as of 1925
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Haiti has two main highways that run from one end of the country to the other. The northern highway, Route Nationale No. 1 (National Highway One), originates in Port-au-Prince, winding through the coastal towns of <a href="/wiki/Montrouis" title="Montrouis">Montrouis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gona%C3%AFves" title="Gonaïves">Gonaïves</a>, before reaching its terminus at the northern port <a href="/wiki/Cap-Ha%C3%AFtien" title="Cap-Haïtien">Cap-Haïtien</a>. The southern highway, Route Nationale No. 2, links Port-au-Prince with <a href="/wiki/Les_Cayes" title="Les Cayes">Les Cayes</a> via <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9og%C3%A2ne" title="Léogâne">Léogâne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petit-Go%C3%A2ve" title="Petit-Goâve">Petit-Goâve</a>. The state of Haiti's roads are generally poor, many being potholed and becoming impassable in rough weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Encylopedia_Britannica_-_Haiti_21-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encylopedia_Britannica_-_Haiti-21">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the Washington Post, "Officials from the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers said Saturday [23 January 2010] that they assessed the damage from the [12 January] quake in Port-au-Prince, Haiti, and found that many of the roads aren't any worse than they were before because they've always been in poor condition."<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[310]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The port at Port-au-Prince, <a href="/wiki/Port_international_de_Port-au-Prince" title="Port international de Port-au-Prince">Port international de Port-au-Prince</a>, has more registered shipping than any of the other dozen ports in the country. The port's facilities include <a href="/wiki/Crane_(machine)" title="Crane (machine)">cranes</a>, large <a href="/wiki/Berth_(moorings)" title="Berth (moorings)">berths</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Warehouse" title="Warehouse">warehouses</a>, but these facilities are not in good condition. The port is underused, possibly due to the substantially high port fees. The port of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Marc" title="Saint-Marc">Saint-Marc</a> is currently the preferred port of entry for consumer goods coming into Haiti. Reasons for this may include its location away from volatile and <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">congested</a> Port-au-Prince, as well as its central location relative to numerous Haitian cities. </p>
      <p>In the past, Haiti used rail transport, however the rail infrastructure was poorly maintained when in use and cost of rehabilitation is beyond the means of the Haitian economy. In 2018 the Regional Development Council of the Dominican Republic proposed a "trans-Hispaniola" railway between both countries.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[311]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airports">Airports</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg/220px-Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg/330px-Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg/440px-Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3373" data-file-height="1940"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Toussaint L'Ouverture International Airport
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Haiti" title="List of airports in Haiti">List of airports in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Toussaint_Louverture_International_Airport" title="Toussaint Louverture International Airport">Toussaint Louverture International Airport</a>, located 10 kilometres (6.2&nbsp;mi) North/North East of Port-au-Prince proper in the commune of <a href="/wiki/Tabarre" title="Tabarre">Tabarre</a>, is the primary transportation hub regarding entry and exit into the country. It has Haiti's main <a href="/wiki/Jetway" class="mw-redirect" title="Jetway">jetway</a>, and along with <a href="/wiki/Cap-Ha%C3%AFtien_International_Airport" title="Cap-Haïtien International Airport">Cap-Haïtien International Airport</a> located near the northern city of Cap-Haïtien, handles the vast majority of the country's international flights. Cities such as Jacmel, Jérémie, Les Cayes, and Port-de-Paix have smaller, less accessible airports that are serviced by <a href="/wiki/Regional_airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional airlines">regional airlines</a> and private aircraft. Such companies include: <a href="/wiki/Caribintair" title="Caribintair">Caribintair</a> (defunct), <a href="/wiki/Sunrise_Airways" title="Sunrise Airways">Sunrise Airways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tortug%27_Air" title="Tortug' Air">Tortug' Air</a> (defunct). </p>
      <p>In 2013, plans for the development of an international airport on Île-à-Vache were introduced by the Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[312]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bus_service">Bus service</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg/220px-Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg/330px-Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg/440px-Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="431"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tap_tap_public_transportation_Haiti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A "Tap tap" bus in <a href="/wiki/Port-Salut" title="Port-Salut">Port-Salut</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Tap_tap" title="Tap tap">Tap tap</a> buses are colorfully painted buses or pick-up trucks that serve as share taxis. The "tap tap" name comes from the sound of passengers tapping on the metal bus body to indicate they want off.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[313]</a></sup> These vehicles for hire are often privately owned and extensively decorated. They follow fixed routes, do not leave until filled with passengers, and riders can usually disembark at any point. The decorations are a typically Haitian form of art.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">[314]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In August 2013, the first coach bus prototype was made in Haiti.<sup id="cite_ref-http_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-http-317">[315]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Haiti" title="Telecommunications in Haiti">Telecommunications in Haiti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Television_in_Haiti" title="Television in Haiti">Television in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In Haiti, communications include the radio, television, fixed and mobile telephones, and the Internet. Haiti ranked last among North American countries in the World Economic Forum's Network Readiness Index (NRI)&nbsp;– an indicator for determining the development level of a country's information and communication technologies. Haiti ranked number 143 out of 148 overall in the 2014 NRI ranking, down from 141 in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">Water supply and sanitation</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Haiti" title="Water supply and sanitation in Haiti">Water supply and sanitation in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Haiti faces key challenges in the <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> sector: Notably, access to public services is very low, their quality is inadequate and public institutions remain very weak despite foreign aid and the government's declared intent to strengthen the sector's institutions. Foreign and Haitian <a href="/wiki/NGO" class="mw-redirect" title="NGO">NGOs</a> play an important role in the sector, especially in rural and urban slum areas. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Haiti" title="Demographics of Haiti">Demographics of Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Haiti-demographie.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Haiti-demographie.png/440px-Haiti-demographie.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Haiti-demographie.png/660px-Haiti-demographie.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Haiti-demographie.png 2x" data-file-width="725" data-file-height="426"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Haiti-demographie.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Haiti's population (1961–2003)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Haiti's population is about 10,788,000 (July 2018 est.)<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti_20-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti-20">[19]</a></sup> with half of the population younger than age 20.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">[317]</a></sup> In 1950, the first formal census gave a total population of 3.1 million.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[318]</a></sup> Haiti averages approximately 350 people per square kilometer (~900 per sq mi.), with its population concentrated most heavily in urban areas, coastal plains, and valleys. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:EscombrosBelAir7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/EscombrosBelAir7.jpg/220px-EscombrosBelAir7.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/EscombrosBelAir7.jpg/330px-EscombrosBelAir7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/EscombrosBelAir7.jpg/440px-EscombrosBelAir7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="1772"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:EscombrosBelAir7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>People in Port-au-Prince
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Most Haitians are descendants of former black <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of Africa">African</a> slaves, including <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">Mulattoes</a> who are <a href="/wiki/Multiracial" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiracial">mixed-race</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti_20-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti-20">[19]</a></sup> The remainder are of <a href="/wiki/White_Haitian" class="mw-redirect" title="White Haitian">European</a> or <a href="/wiki/Arab_Haitians" title="Arab Haitians">Arab descent</a>, the descendants of settlers (colonial remnants and contemporary immigration during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-joshuaproject.net_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-joshuaproject.net-321">[319]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[320]</a></sup> Haitians of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> descent or <a href="/wiki/Marabou_(ethnicity)" title="Marabou (ethnicity)">East Indian</a> origin number approximately more than 400.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Millions of Haitians live abroad in the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Americans" title="Haitian Americans">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Cuban" title="Haitian Cuban">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (primarily <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bahamas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Antilles" class="mw-redirect" title="French Antilles">French Antilles</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Turks_and_Caicos" class="mw-redirect" title="Turks and Caicos">Turks and Caicos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>. There are an estimated 881,500 Haitians in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[321]</a></sup> 800,000 in the Dominican Republic,<sup id="cite_ref-pinadep_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinadep-324">[322]</a></sup> 300,000 in Cuba,<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[323]</a></sup> 100,000 in Canada,<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[324]</a></sup> 80,000 in France,<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[325]</a></sup> and up to 80,000 in the Bahamas.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[326]</a></sup> There are also smaller Haitian communities in many other countries, including <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. </p>
      <p>In 2018, the life expectancy at birth was 63.66 years.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[327]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_genetics">Population genetics</span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Autosomal_DNA">Autosomal DNA</span></h4> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pool</a> of Haiti is about 95.5% <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan African</a>, 4.3% <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">European</a>, with the rest showing some traces of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> genes;<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-330">[328]</a></sup> according to a 2010 <a href="/wiki/Autosomal" class="mw-redirect" title="Autosomal">autosomal</a> <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">genealogical DNA testing</a>. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Y-chromosome_and_mitochondrial_DNA">Y-chromosome and mitochondrial DNA</span></h4> 
      <table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/44px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="44" height="31" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/66px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/88px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31"></a>
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section <b>needs expansion</b> with: information about <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroups</a>, which assesses the DNA inherited by <a href="/wiki/Matriline" class="mw-redirect" title="Matriline">matriline</a>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Haiti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2015</span>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>A 2012 genetic study on Haitian and Jamaican <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">Y-chromosomal ancestry</a> has revealed that both populations "exhibit a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_populations_of_Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Y-DNA haplogroups in populations of Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan paternal component</a>, with haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_A_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup A (Y-DNA)">A1b-V152</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_A_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup A (Y-DNA)">A3-M32</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_B_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup B (Y-DNA)">B2-M182</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup E (Y-DNA)">E1a-M33</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup E (Y-DNA)">E1b1a-M2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup E (Y-DNA)">E2b-M98</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R1b_(Y-DNA)#R1b1c_(R-V88)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup R1b (Y-DNA)">R1b2-V88</a>" comprising (77.2%) of the Haitian and (66.7%) of Jamaican paternal gene pools.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-331">[329]</a></sup> Y-chromosomes indicative of <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_populations_of_Europe" title="Y-DNA haplogroups in populations of Europe">European ancestry</a> "(i.e., haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_G-M201" title="Haplogroup G-M201">G2a*-P15</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_I-M170" title="Haplogroup I-M170">I-M258</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R1b_(Y-DNA)#R1b1a2_(R-M269)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup R1b (Y-DNA)">R1b1b-M269</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_T-M184" title="Haplogroup T-M184">T-M184</a>) were detected at commensurate levels in Haiti (20.3%) and Jamaica (18.9%)".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_331-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-331">[329]</a></sup> While Y-haplogroups indicative of Chinese <a href="/wiki/O-M175" class="mw-redirect" title="O-M175">O-M175</a> (3.8%) and Indian <a href="/wiki/H-M69" class="mw-redirect" title="H-M69">H-M69</a> (0.6%) and <a href="/wiki/L-M20" class="mw-redirect" title="L-M20">L-M20</a> (0.6%) ancestry were found at significant levels in Jamaica,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_331-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-331">[329]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arab_Haitian" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab Haitian">Levantine</a> Y-haplogroups were found in Haiti. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Duffy_antigens">Duffy antigens</span></h4> 
      <p>According to a 2008 study examining the frequency of the <a href="/wiki/Duffy_antigen_system" title="Duffy antigen system">Duffy antigen</a> receptor for <a href="/wiki/Chemokine" title="Chemokine">Chemokines</a> (<a href="/wiki/DARC_(gene)" class="mw-redirect" title="DARC (gene)">DARC</a>) <a href="/wiki/Single-nucleotide_polymorphism" title="Single-nucleotide polymorphism">Single Nucleotide polymorphisms</a> (SNPs), (75%) of Haitian women sampled exhibited the CC genotype (absent among women of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> ancestry) at levels comparable to US <a href="/wiki/African-Americans" class="mw-redirect" title="African-Americans">African-Americans</a> (73%), but more than <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaican</a> females (63%).<sup id="cite_ref-Genetic-structure_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetic-structure-332">[330]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">[331]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racial_discrimination">Racial discrimination</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gens_de_couleur" class="mw-redirect" title="Gens de couleur">Gens de couleur</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Under colonial rule, <a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">Haitian mulattoes</a> were generally privileged above the black majority, though they possessed fewer rights than the white population. Following the country's independence, they became the nation's social elite. Numerous leaders throughout Haiti's history have been mulattoes. During this time, the slaves and the <a href="/wiki/Affranchi" title="Affranchi">affranchis</a> were given limited opportunities toward education, income, and occupations, but even after gaining independence, the social structure remains a legacy today as the disparity between the upper and lower classes have not been reformed significantly since the colonial days.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[332]</a></sup> Comprising 5% of the nation's population, mulattoes have retained their preeminence, evident in the political, economic, social and cultural hierarchy in Haiti.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">[333]</a></sup> As a result, the elite class today consists of a small group of influential people who are generally light in color and continue to establish themselves in high, prestigious positions.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">[334]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Haiti" title="Religion in Haiti">Religion in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px"> 
        <div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black"> 
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:Red"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 524.21835811132px; border-left-color:gray"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:gray"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:gray"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 87.051316381041px; border-left-color:Blue"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Blue"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:Blue"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:41.437558099328px 91.0105970685px 0 0; border-top-color:purple"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:purple"></div> 
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption"> 
         <p>Religion in Haiti according to the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">[335]</a></sup> </p> 
         <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style>
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:purple; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Catholicism (56.8%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:Blue; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Protestantism (29.6%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:gray; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Unaffiliated (10.6%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:Red; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Other (3%)
         </div> 
        </div> 
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The 2017 CIA Factbook reported that around 54.7% of Haitians profess to being <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Haiti" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Haiti">Catholics</a> while <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Haiti" title="Protestantism in Haiti">Protestants</a> made up about 28.5% of the population (Baptist 15.4%, Pentecostal 7.9%, <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh-day Adventist">Seventh-day Adventist</a> 3%, Methodist 1.5%, other 0.7%). Other sources put the Protestant population higher than this, suggesting that it might have formed one-third of the population in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-ReyStepick2013_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReyStepick2013-338">[336]</a></sup> Like other countries in Latin America, Haiti has witnessed a general Protestant expansion, which is largely <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Protestant">Evangelical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a> in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">[337]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">[338]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">[339]</a></sup> Haitian <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_(Catholicism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal (Catholicism)">Cardinal</a> <a href="/wiki/Chibly_Langlois" title="Chibly Langlois">Chibly Langlois</a> is president of the National Bishops Conference of the Catholic Church. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Haitian_vodou" class="mw-redirect" title="Haitian vodou">Vodou</a>, a religion with West African roots similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, originated during colonial times in which slaves were obliged to disguise their <a href="/wiki/Loa" title="Loa">loa</a>, or spirits, as <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> saints, an element of a process called <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> and is still practiced by some Haitians today. Due to the religious syncretism between Catholicism and Vodou, it is difficult to estimate the number of Vodouists in Haiti.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">[340]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">[341]</a></sup> The religion has historically been persecuted and misrepresented in popular media, however in 2003 the Government recognized the faith as an official religion of the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti_20-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_–_Haiti-20">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Minority religions in Haiti include <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Haiti" title="Islam in Haiti">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith_in_Haiti" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahá'í Faith in Haiti">Bahá'í Faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Haiti" title="History of the Jews in Haiti">Judaism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_20110303_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_20110303-4">[4]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3> 
      <p>The two official languages of Haiti are <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole" title="Haitian Creole">Haitian Creole</a>. French is the principal written and administratively authorized language (as well as the main language of the press) and is spoken by 42% of Haitians.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">[342]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">[343]</a></sup> It is spoken by all educated Haitians, is the medium of instruction in most schools, and is used in the business sector. It is also used in ceremonial events such as weddings, graduations and church Masses. Haiti is one of two independent nations in the Americas (along with Canada) to designate French as an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>; the other French-speaking areas are all <a href="/wiki/French_overseas_departments_and_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="French overseas departments and territories">overseas</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">départements</a></i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Overseas_collectivity" title="Overseas collectivity">collectivités</a>,</i> of France, such as <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>. </p>
      <p>Haitian Creole,<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">[344]</a></sup> which has recently undergone a standardization, is spoken by virtually the entire population of Haiti.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">[345]</a></sup> Haitian Creole is one of the <a href="/wiki/French-based_creole_languages" title="French-based creole languages">French-based creole languages</a>. Its vocabulary is 90% derived from French, but its grammar resembles that of some West African languages. It also has influences from Taino, Spanish, and Portuguese.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">[346]</a></sup> Haitian Creole is related to the other <a href="/wiki/French-based_creole_languages" title="French-based creole languages">French creoles</a>, but most closely to the <a href="/wiki/Antillean_Creole" title="Antillean Creole">Antillean Creole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Louisiana Creole French">Louisiana Creole</a> variants. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emigration">Emigration</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haitian_diaspora" title="Haitian diaspora">Haitian diaspora</a>
      </div> 
      <p>There is a large Haitian diaspora community, predominantly based in the US and Canada, France, and the wealthier Caribbean islands. </p>
      <p>Emigrants from Haiti have constituted a segment of American and Canadian society since before <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">the independence of Haiti from France</a> in 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-HammondCanadaHaiti2010_349-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HammondCanadaHaiti2010-349">[347]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PBSResourceBank_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSResourceBank-350">[348]</a></sup> Many influential early American settlers and black freemen, including <a href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_Point_du_Sable" title="Jean Baptiste Point du Sable">Jean Baptiste Point du Sable</a> and <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a>, were of Haitian origin.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinzie_1856_190_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinzie_1856_190-351">[349]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meehan_1963_445_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meehan_1963_445-352">[350]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cohn2009_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn2009-353">[351]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewis18_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis18-354">[352]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Jean Baptiste Point du Sable, an immigrant from <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a> (now the Republic of Haiti), founded the first nonindigenous settlement in what is now <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Illinois">Chicago, Illinois</a>, the third largest city in the United States. The state of Illinois and city of Chicago declared du Sable the founder of Chicago on 26 October 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinzie_1856_190_351-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinzie_1856_190-351">[349]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meehan_1963_445_352-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meehan_1963_445-352">[350]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cohn2009_353-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn2009-353">[351]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_cities">Largest cities</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Haiti" title="List of cities in Haiti">List of cities in Haiti</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="navbox"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th colspan="10" class="navbox-title">
